# big-code-django-arch-001: Django ORM Query Compilation Pipeline

## Task

Map the Django ORM query compilation pipeline from QuerySet to SQL. Trace how a high-level QuerySet API call (e.g., `Model.objects.filter(...)`) is lazily constructed, compiled into SQL, and executed against the database, including the expression/lookup system and backend-specific vendor dispatch.

## Context

- **Repository**: django/django (Python, ~350K LOC)
- **Category**: Architectural Understanding
- **Difficulty**: hard
- **Subsystem Focus**: django/db/models/ and django/db/backends/ — the ORM query compilation layer

## Requirements

1. Identify all files involved in the query compilation pipeline (Manager, QuerySet, Query, Compiler, Expressions, Lookups, Backend)
2. Trace the dependency chain from `Manager.get_queryset()` through lazy query building, compilation via `as_sql()`, to `execute_sql()`
3. Document the `compile(node)` vendor dispatch mechanism (`as_{vendor}()` pattern)
4. Explain how the WhereNode tree structure, Expression protocol, and Lookup system compose to produce SQL

## Expected Output

Write your analysis to `/logs/agent/solution.md` with the following structure:

```
## Files Examined
- path/to/file1.ext — role in architecture
- path/to/file2.ext — role in architecture
...

## Dependency Chain
1. Entry point: path/to/entry.ext
2. Calls: path/to/next.ext (via function/method name)
3. Delegates to: path/to/impl.ext
...

## Analysis
[Detailed architectural analysis including:
- Design patterns identified
- Component responsibilities
- Data flow description
- Interface contracts between components]

## Summary
[Concise 2-3 sentence summary answering the task question]
```

## Evaluation Criteria

- File recall: Did you find the correct set of architecturally relevant files?
- Dependency accuracy: Did you trace the correct dependency/call chain?
- Architectural coherence: Did you correctly identify the design patterns and component relationships?

772026-03-11T03:41:38.939764agentmessage-
I am thinking...: Now I have a comprehensive understanding of the entire Django ORM query compilation pipeline. Let me organize all the information I've gathered to write a thorough architectural analysis.

Key components and their relationships:

1. **Manager layer** (`django/db/models/manager.py`)
   - `BaseManager` - base class with `get_queryset()` returning `QuerySet(model=self.model, using=self._db, hints=self._hints)`
   - `Manager = BaseManager.from_queryset(QuerySet)` - dynamically creates a class that proxies QuerySet methods
   - `from_queryset()` uses `type()` to create a new class with `_queryset_class` and all QuerySet methods copied over
   - `ManagerDescriptor` - descriptor that returns the manager when accessed on the class

2. **QuerySet layer** (`django/db/models/query.py`)
   - `QuerySet.__init__` creates `sql.Query(self.model)` stored as `self._query`
   - `_iterable_class = ModelIterable` by default
   - `filter()` → `_filter_or_exclude(False, args, kwargs)` → `_filter_or_exclude_inplace()` → `self._query.add_q(Q(*args, **kwargs))`
   - `exclude()` → `_filter_or_exclude(True, ...)` → `self._query.add_q(~Q(...))`
   - `__iter__()` → `_fetch_all()` → `list(self._iterable_class(self))`
   - `_fetch_all()` → `list(ModelIterable(self))`
   - `ModelIterable.__iter__()` → `query.get_compiler(using=db)` → `compiler.execute_sql(...)` → `compiler.results_iter(results)` → yields model instances

3. **Query layer** (`django/db/models/sql/query.py`)
   - `Query(BaseExpression)` - the internal SQL query representation
   - `Query.compiler = "SQLCompiler"` - class attribute naming the compiler
   - `Query.__init__` creates `self.where = WhereNode()`
   - `add_q(q_object)` → `_add_q(q_object, can_reuse)` → `self.where.add(clause, AND)`
   - `_add_q()` iterates Q c
... (truncated)

"def results_iter(\n        self,\n        results=None,\n        tuple_expected=False,\n        chunked_fetch=False,\n        chunk_size=GET_ITERATOR_CHUNK_SIZE,\n    ):\n        \"\"\"Return an iterator over the results from executing this query.\"\"\"\n        if results is None:\n            results = self.execute_sql(\n                MULTI, chunked_fetch=chunked_fetch, chunk_size=chunk_size\n            )\n        fields = [s[0] for s in self.select[0 : self.col_count]]\n        converters = self.get_converters(fields)\n        rows = chain.from_iterable(results)\n        if converters:\n            rows = self.apply_converters(rows, converters)\n        if self.has_composite_fields(fields):\n            rows = self.composite_fields_to_tuples(rows, fields)\n        if tuple_expected:\n            rows = map(tuple, rows)\n        return rows\n\n    def has_results(self):\n        \"\"\"\n        Backends (e.g. NoSQL) can override this in order to use optimized\n        versions of \"query has any results.\"\n        \"\"\"\n        return bool(self.execute_sql(SINGLE))\n\n    def execute_sql(\n        self, result_type=MULTI, chunked_fetch=False, chunk_size=GET_ITERATOR_CHUNK_SIZE\n    ):\n        \"\"\"\n        Run the query against the database and return the result(s).   "file_text": "# Django ORM Query Compilation Pipeline: Architectural Analysis\n\n## Overview\n\nDjango's ORM translates Python QuerySet operations into SQL through a multi-stage pipeline. The design is deliberately lazy: no SQL is generated until results are actually needed. The pipeline separates concerns across four distinct layers \u2014 the public API (Manager/QuerySet), the internal query representation (Query/WhereNode), the SQL compiler (SQLCompiler), and the expression/lookup system (Expression/Lookup). A vendor dispatch mechanism threads through the entire compilation stage to allow backend-specific SQL generation without subclassing the core compiler.\n\n---\n\n## Files Involved\n\n| File | Role |\n|---|---|\n| `django/db/models/manager.py` | `Manager` / `BaseManager` \u2014 entry point, creates QuerySet |\n| `django/db/models/query.py` | `QuerySet`, `ModelIterable` \u2014 public API, lazy evaluation |\n| `django/db/models/sql/query.py` | `Query` \u2014 internal SQL representation, filter tree construction |\n| `django/db/models/sql/compiler.py` | `SQLCompiler` \u2014 SQL generation and execution |\n| `django/db/models/sql/where.py` | `WhereNode` \u2014 boolean condition tree |\n| `django/db/models/sql/datastructures.py` | `Join`, `BaseTable` \u2014 FROM clause structures |\n| `django/db/models/expressions.py` | `BaseExpression`, `Col`, `F`, `Value`, `Func`, `Subquery` |\n| `django/db/models/lookups.py` | `Lookup`, `BuiltinLookup`, `Transform`, concrete lookup classes |\n| `django/db/models/query_utils.py` | `Q`, `RegisterLookupMixin` |\n| `django/utils/tree.py` | `Node` \u2014 generic tree base for `Q` and `WhereNode` |\n| `django/db/backends/base/operations.py` | `BaseDatabaseOperations.compiler()` \u2014 compiler resolution |\n| `django/db/backends/postgresql/compiler.py` | PostgreSQL-specific compiler overrides |\n| `django/db/backends/mysql/compiler.py` | MySQL-specific compiler overrides |\n\n---\n\n## Stage 1: Manager \u2192 QuerySet (Lazy Initialization)\n\n### `Manager.get_queryset()` \u2014 `django/db/models/manager.py:150`\n\n```python\ndef get_queryset(self):\n    return self._queryset_class(model=self.model, using=self._db, hints=self._hints)\n```\n\n`Manager` is defined as `BaseManager.from_queryset(QuerySet)`. The `from_queryset()` class method uses `type()` to dynamically create a new class that inherits from `BaseManager` and copies all public `QuerySet` methods as proxy methods onto the manager. This means `Model.objects.filter(...)` is equivalent to `Model.objects.get_queryset().filter(...)`.\n\n`ManagerDescriptor` is a descriptor placed on the model class. When accessed on the class (not an instance), it returns the manager. When accessed on an instance, it raises `AttributeError`, enforcing that managers are class-level constructs.\n\n### `QuerySet.__init__()` \u2014 `django/db/models/query.py:280`\n\n```python\ndef __init__(self, model=None, query=None, using=None, hints=None):\n    self.model = model\n    self._db = using\n    self._query = query or sql.Query(self.model)\n    self._result_cache = None\n    self._iterable_class = ModelIterable\n    ...\n```\n\nThe QuerySet wraps a `sql.Query` object (the internal representation) and sets `_result_cache = None`. No SQL is generated here. The `_iterable_class` defaults to `ModelIterable`; it switches to `ValuesIterable`, `ValuesListIterable`, etc. when `.values()` or `.values_list()` is called.\n\n**Key design point:** `QuerySet` is a lazy wrapper. Every chaining method (`.filter()`, `.exclude()`, `.order_by()`, `.select_related()`) returns a new cloned QuerySet with the internal `Query` object mutated to reflect the new constraint. SQL is never generated during this phase.\n\n---\n\n## Stage 2: Filter Application \u2014 Q Objects and WhereNode Construction\n\n### `QuerySet.filter()` \u2192 `_filter_or_exclude()` \u2014 `django/db/models/query.py:1475`\n\n```python\ndef filter(self, *args, **kwargs):\n    return self._filter_or_exclude(False, args, kwargs)\n\ndef _filter_or_exclude(self, negate, args, kwargs):\n    clone = self._chain()\n    clone._filter_or_exclude_inplace(negate, args, kwargs)\n    return clone\n\ndef _filter_or_exclude_inplace(self, negate, args, kwargs):\n    if negate:\n        self._query.add_q(~Q(*args, **kwargs))\n    else:\n        self._query.add_q(Q(*args, **kwargs))\n```\n\n`filter()` clones the QuerySet and delegates to `Query.add_q()`. `exclude()` does the same but wraps the `Q` in a negation (`~Q`). The clone ensures immutability of the original QuerySet.\n\n### `Q` \u2014 `django/db/models/query_utils.py:39`\n\n`Q` subclasses `django.utils.tree.Node`, which is a generic n-ary tree node. Each `Q` object has:\n- `children`: a list of `(lookup_string, value)` tuples or nested `Q` objects\n- `connector`: `AND`, `OR`, or `XOR`\n- `negated`: boolean\n\n```python\nclass Q(tree.Node):\n    AND = \"AND\"\n    OR = \"OR\"\n    XOR = \"XOR\"\n    default = AND\n\n    def __init__(self, *args, _connector=None, _negated=False, **kwargs):\n        super().__init__(\n            children=[*args, *sorted(kwargs.items())],\n            connector=_connector,\n            negated=_negated,\n        )\n```\n\n`Q` objects support `&`, `|`, `^`, and `~` operators, which produce new `Q` nodes with the appropriate connector or negation. This allows complex filter expressions to be composed before being handed to the query.\n\n### `Query.add_q()` \u2192 `_add_q()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/query.py:1625`\n\n```python\ndef add_q(self, q_object, reuse_all=False):\n    existing_inner = {a for a in self.alias_map if self.alias_map[a].join_type == INNER}\n    can_reuse = self.used_aliases\n    clause, _ = self._add_q(q_object, can_reuse)\n    if clause:\n        self.where.add(clause, AND)\n    self.demote_joins(existing_inner)\n```\n\n`add_q()` is the preprocessor. It calls `_add_q()` to recursively walk the `Q` tree and build a `WhereNode` subtree, then ANDs that subtree into `self.where` (the root `WhereNode` of the query). After adding, it calls `demote_joins()` to convert any OUTER JOINs that are now constrained back to INNER JOINs.\n\n```python\ndef _add_q(self, q_object, used_aliases, branch_negated=False,\n           current_negated=False, allow_joins=True, ...):\n    connector = q_object.connector\n    current_negated ^= q_object.negated\n    target_clause = WhereNode(connector=connector, negated=q_object.negated)\n    for child in q_object.children:\n        child_clause, needed_inner = self.build_filter(child, ...)\n        if child_clause:\n            target_clause.add(child_clause, connector)\n    return target_clause, needed_inner\n```\n\n`_add_q()` creates a new `WhereNode` mirroring the `Q` node's connector and negation, then calls `build_filter()` for each child, adding the resulting `WhereNode` clauses as children.\n\n### `Query.build_filter()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/query.py:1460`\n\nThis is the core of filter-to-SQL-tree translation. For a `(lookup_string, value)` tuple like `('author__name__icontains', 'Django')`:\n\n1. **`solve_lookup_type(arg)`** \u2014 splits `'author__name__icontains'` into field parts `['author', 'name']` and lookup parts `['icontains']` by walking `names_to_path()` against the model's `_meta`.\n\n2. **`setup_joins(parts, opts, alias)`** \u2014 traverses the field path, creating JOIN entries in `query.alias_map` for each relation traversed. Returns `join_info` containing the final field, alias, and join list.\n\n3. **`trim_joins()`** \u2014 removes unnecessary joins (e.g., when joining to a primary key that can be resolved on the parent table).\n\n4. **`_get_col(target, field, alias)`** \u2014 creates a `Col(alias, target)` expression representing the left-hand side of the lookup.\n\n5. **`build_lookup(lookups, col, value)`** \u2014 resolves the lookup class and instantiates it:\n\n```python\ndef build_lookup(self, lookups, lhs, rhs):\n    *transforms, lookup_name = lookups or [\"exact\"]\n    for name in transforms:\n        lhs = self.try_transform(lhs, name, lookups)\n    lookup_class = lhs.get_lookup(lookup_name)\n    lookup = lookup_class(lhs, rhs)\n    return lookup\n```\n\nTransforms (e.g., `__year`, `__month`) are applied first, each wrapping the previous `lhs` in a `Transform` instance. Then the final lookup class is retrieved via `RegisterLookupMixin.get_lookup()` and instantiated with `(lhs, rhs)`.\n\n6. Returns `WhereNode([lookup_instance], connector=AND)`.\n\n---\n\n## Stage 3: Lazy Evaluation Trigger \u2014 `_fetch_all()` and `ModelIterable`\n\n### `QuerySet.__iter__()` \u2192 `_fetch_all()` \u2014 `django/db/models/query.py:369`\n\n```python\ndef __iter__(self):\n    self._fetch_all()\n    return iter(self._result_cache)\n\ndef _fetch_all(self):\n    if self._result_cache is None:\n        self._result_cache = list(self._iterable_class(self))\n```\n\nIteration is the primary trigger for SQL execution. `_fetch_all()` checks `_result_cache`; if `None`, it materializes the QuerySet by consuming `ModelIterable`. Other triggers include `bool()`, `len()`, slicing with `__getitem__`, and explicit `.iterator()` calls.\n\n### `ModelIterable.__iter__()` \u2014 `django/db/models/query.py:85`\n\n```python\ndef __iter__(self):\n    queryset = self.queryset\n    db = queryset.db\n    compiler = queryset.query.get_compiler(using=db)\n    results = compiler.execute_sql(chunked_fetch=self.chunked_fetch, chunk_size=self.chunk_size)\n    ...\n    for row in compiler.results_iter(results):\n        obj = model_cls.from_db(db, init_list, row[model_fields_start:model_fields_end])\n        ...\n        yield obj\n```\n\n`ModelIterable` is the bridge between the QuerySet API and the SQL compiler. It:\n1. Calls `query.get_compiler()` to obtain the appropriate `SQLCompiler` instance.\n2. Calls `compiler.execute_sql()` to run the query and get raw rows.\n3. Calls `compiler.results_iter()` to iterate over rows with type converters applied.\n4. Calls `model_cls.from_db()` to construct model instances from each row.\n\n---\n\n## Stage 4: Compiler Resolution \u2014 `get_compiler()` and `BaseDatabaseOperations.compiler()`\n\n### `Query.get_compiler()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/query.py:358`\n\n```python\ndef get_compiler(self, using=None, connection=None, elide_empty=True):\n    if using:\n        connection = connections[using]\n    return connection.ops.compiler(self.compiler)(self, connection, using, elide_empty)\n```\n\n`Query.compiler = \"SQLCompiler\"` is a class attribute. `connection.ops` is the backend's `DatabaseOperations` instance. This call resolves the compiler class by name from the backend's compiler module and instantiates it with the query, connection, database alias, and `elide_empty` flag.\n\n### `BaseDatabaseOperations.compiler()` \u2014 `django/db/backends/base/operations.py:385`\n\n```python\ncompiler_module = \"django.db.models.sql.compiler\"\n\ndef compiler(self, compiler_name):\n    if self._cache is None:\n        self._cache = import_module(self.compiler_module)\n    return getattr(self._cache, compiler_name)\n```\n\nThe `compiler_module` attribute is the extension point for backend-specific compilers:\n\n| Backend | `vendor` | `compiler_module` |\n|---|---|---|\n| SQLite | `\"sqlite\"` | `\"django.db.models.sql.compiler\"` (default) |\n| PostgreSQL | `\"postgresql\"` | `\"django.db.backends.postgresql.compiler\"` |\n| MySQL | `\"mysql\"` | `\"django.db.backends.mysql.compiler\"` |\n| Oracle | `\"oracle\"` | `\"django.db.models.sql.compiler\"` (default) |\n\nPostgreSQL overrides `SQLInsertCompiler` to use `UNNEST` for bulk inserts. MySQL overrides `SQLDeleteCompiler` and `SQLUpdateCompiler` to handle multi-table delete/update syntax. SQLite and Oracle use the base compiler for all operations, relying instead on vendor dispatch within expressions.\n\n---\n\n## Stage 5: SQL Assembly \u2014 `SQLCompiler.as_sql()`\n\n### `SQLCompiler.__init__()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/compiler.py`\n\n```python\ndef __init__(self, query, connection, using, elide_empty=True):\n    self.query = query\n    self.connection = connection\n    self.using = using\n    self.elide_empty = elide_empty\n    self.quote_cache = {\"*\": \"*\"}\n    self.select = None\n    self.annotation_col_map = None\n    self.klass_info = None\n    self.where = None\n    self.having = None\n    self.qualify = None\n```\n\n### `SQLCompiler.as_sql()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/compiler.py:754`\n\n`as_sql()` orchestrates the full SQL string assembly:\n\n```python\ndef as_sql(self, with_limits=True, with_col_aliases=False):\n    extra_select, order_by, group_by = self.pre_sql_setup(...)\n    ...\n    distinct_fields, distinct_params = self.get_distinct()\n    from_, f_params = self.get_from_clause()\n    where, w_params = self.compile(self.where) if self.where else (\"\", [])\n    having, h_params = self.compile(self.having) if self.having else (\"\", [])\n\n    result = [\"SELECT\"]\n    result += [\", \".join(out_cols)]   # compiled column expressions\n    result += [\"FROM\", *from_]        # compiled JOIN clauses\n    if where:\n        result.append(\"WHERE %s\" % where)\n    if grouping:\n        result.append(\"GROUP BY %s\" % \", \".join(grouping))\n    if having:\n        result.append(\"HAVING %s\" % having)\n    if order_by:\n        result.append(\"ORDER BY %s\" % \", \".join(ordering))\n    ...\n    return \" \".join(result), tuple(params)\n```\n\n**`pre_sql_setup()`** calls `get_select()` (which compiles all SELECT column expressions via `compile(col)`), `get_order_by()`, `where.split_having_qualify()` (which partitions the `WhereNode` tree into WHERE/HAVING/QUALIFY parts), and `get_group_by()`.\n\n**`get_from_clause()`** iterates `query.alias_map`, calling `compile(from_clause)` for each referenced alias. `Join.as_sql()` and `BaseTable.as_sql()` in `sql/datastructures.py` produce the `LEFT OUTER JOIN ... ON ...` and bare table name strings respectively.\n\n**`get_select()`** compiles each column expression via `compile(col)`, where `col` is typically a `Col` instance. The result is a list of `(expression, (sql, params), alias)` triples stored in `self.select`.\n\n---\n\n## Stage 6: The `compile(node)` Vendor Dispatch Mechanism\n\n### `SQLCompiler.compile()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/compiler.py:571`\n\n```python\ndef compile(self, node):\n    vendor_impl = getattr(node, \"as_\" + self.connection.vendor, None)\n    if vendor_impl:\n        sql, params = vendor_impl(self, self.connection)\n    else:\n        sql, params = node.as_sql(self, self.connection)\n    return sql, params\n```\n\nThis is the central dispatch point of the entire compilation pipeline. Every node in the expression tree \u2014 `WhereNode`, `Lookup`, `Col`, `Func`, `Join`, `OrderBy`, etc. \u2014 is compiled through this single method.\n\n**Dispatch logic:**\n1. Check if the node has a method named `as_{vendor}` (e.g., `as_sqlite`, `as_postgresql`, `as_mysql`, `as_oracle`).\n2. If found, call it with `(compiler, connection)` \u2014 the vendor-specific implementation takes precedence.\n3. If not found, fall back to the generic `node.as_sql(compiler, connection)`.\n\nThis design allows any expression class to provide backend-specific SQL generation by simply defining an `as_{vendor}` method, without requiring subclassing of the compiler or any central dispatch table. The compiler itself remains backend-agnostic.\n\n**Examples of vendor-specific implementations:**\n\n- `SQLiteNumericMixin.as_sqlite()` (`expressions.py:22`): Wraps `DecimalField` expressions in `CAST(... AS NUMERIC)` because SQLite stores decimals as text and needs explicit casting for arithmetic.\n- `Lookup.as_oracle()` (`lookups.py:152`): Wraps boolean expressions in `CASE WHEN ... THEN True ELSE False END` because Oracle does not support boolean expressions directly in WHERE clauses.\n- `Func` subclasses in `django/db/models/functions/`: Many database functions have vendor-specific SQL syntax (e.g., `NOW()` vs `GETDATE()`, `SUBSTR` vs `SUBSTRING`).\n- JSON field lookups in `django/db/models/fields/json.py`: All four vendor methods are defined because JSON path syntax differs significantly across databases.\n\n---\n\n## Stage 7: WhereNode and Expression Tree Compilation\n\n### `WhereNode.as_sql()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/where.py:116`\n\n```python\ndef as_sql(self, compiler, connection):\n    result = []\n    result_params = []\n    if self.connector == AND:\n        full_needed, empty_needed = len(self.children), 1\n    else:\n        full_needed, empty_needed = 1, len(self.children)\n\n    for child in self.children:\n        try:\n            sql, params = compiler.compile(child)\n        except EmptyResultSet:\n            empty_needed -= 1\n        except FullResultSet:\n            full_needed -= 1\n        else:\n            if sql:\n                result.append(sql)\n                result_params.extend(params)\n\n    conn = \" %s \" % self.connector\n    sql_string = conn.join(result)\n    if self.negated:\n        sql_string = \"NOT (%s)\" % sql_string\n    elif len(result) > 1 or self.resolved:\n        sql_string = \"(%s)\" % sql_string\n    return sql_string, result_params\n```\n\n`WhereNode.as_sql()` is called by `compiler.compile(self.where)`. It recursively compiles each child by calling `compiler.compile(child)` \u2014 which re-enters the vendor dispatch mechanism for each child node. Children are joined with the connector string (` AND `, ` OR `, ` XOR `). Negation wraps the result in `NOT (...)`.\n\n**Short-circuit semantics:** `EmptyResultSet` and `FullResultSet` exceptions propagate upward through the tree. For an AND node, a single `EmptyResultSet` child causes the whole node to raise `EmptyResultSet` (no rows can match). For an OR node, a single `FullResultSet` child causes the whole node to raise `FullResultSet` (all rows match). This allows Django to elide entire WHERE clauses or return empty results without hitting the database.\n\n**XOR handling:** When the database does not support logical XOR (`connection.features.supports_logical_xor` is False), `WhereNode` rewrites `a XOR b XOR c` as `(a OR b OR c) AND MOD(a + b + c, 2) = 1`.\n\n**`split_having_qualify()`** partitions the `WhereNode` tree into three sub-trees before `as_sql()` is called:\n- **WHERE**: non-aggregate, non-window conditions\n- **HAVING**: conditions involving aggregate expressions (`contains_aggregate = True`)\n- **QUALIFY**: conditions involving window functions (`contains_over_clause = True`)\n\n### `NothingNode` and `ExtraWhere`\n\n`NothingNode.as_sql()` always raises `EmptyResultSet`, used when a filter is known at query-build time to match nothing (e.g., `pk__in=[]`).\n\n`ExtraWhere.as_sql()` returns raw SQL strings injected via `.extra(where=[...])`, bypassing the expression system entirely.\n\n---\n\n## Stage 8: Lookup Compilation \u2014 `Lookup`, `BuiltinLookup`, and Concrete Lookups\n\n### `Lookup` \u2014 `django/db/models/lookups.py:31`\n\n`Lookup(Expression)` is the base class for all comparison operations. It holds:\n- `lhs`: the left-hand side \u2014 typically a `Col` expression (a column reference) or a `Transform`\n- `rhs`: the right-hand side \u2014 a Python value, a `Value` expression, or a subquery `Query`\n\n```python\nclass Lookup(Expression):\n    lookup_name = None\n\n    def __init__(self, lhs, rhs):\n        self.lhs, self.rhs = lhs, rhs\n        self.rhs = self.get_prep_lookup()   # type coercion via output_field\n        self.lhs = self.get_prep_lhs()\n```\n\n**`process_lhs(compiler, connection)`** resolves and compiles the LHS:\n```python\ndef process_lhs(self, compiler, connection, lhs=None):\n    lhs = lhs or self.lhs\n    if hasattr(lhs, \"resolve_expression\"):\n        lhs = lhs.resolve_expression(compiler.query)\n    sql, params = compiler.compile(lhs)\n    return sql, params\n```\n\n**`process_rhs(compiler, connection)`** compiles the RHS. If `rhs` has `as_sql` (i.e., it is an expression or subquery), it calls `compiler.compile(value)`. Otherwise it calls `get_db_prep_lookup()` which returns `(\"%s\", [value])` \u2014 a parameterized placeholder.\n\n### `BuiltinLookup.as_sql()` \u2014 `django/db/models/lookups.py:256`\n\n```python\ndef as_sql(self, compiler, connection):\n    lhs_sql, params = self.process_lhs(compiler, connection)\n    rhs_sql, rhs_params = self.process_rhs(compiler, connection)\n    params.extend(rhs_params)\n    rhs_sql = self.get_rhs_op(connection, rhs_sql)\n    return \"%s %s\" % (lhs_sql, rhs_sql), params\n```\n\n`get_rhs_op()` returns the operator string from `connection.operators`, e.g., `\"= %s\"` for `exact`, `\"> %s\"` for `gt`, `\"LIKE %s\"` for `contains`. The final SQL fragment is `\"table\".\"column\" = %s` with the value in `params`.\n\n`BuiltinLookup.process_lhs()` additionally applies `connection.ops.lookup_cast()`, which wraps the LHS in a database-specific cast when needed (e.g., `UPPER(%s)` for case-insensitive lookups on some backends).\n\n### Concrete Lookup Classes\n\nAll concrete lookups are registered on `Field` via `@Field.register_lookup`:\n\n| Class | `lookup_name` | Generated SQL fragment |\n|---|---|---|\n| `Exact` | `exact` | `col = %s` |\n| `IExact` | `iexact` | `UPPER(col) = UPPER(%s)` |\n| `GreaterThan` | `gt` | `col > %s` |\n| `LessThan` | `lt` | `col < %s` |\n| `In` | `in` | `col IN (%s, %s, ...)` |\n| `IsNull` | `isnull` | `col IS NULL` / `col IS NOT NULL` |\n| `Contains` | `contains` | `col LIKE %s` (with `%value%`) |\n| `Regex` | `regex` | backend-specific regex operator |\n| `Range` | `range` | `col BETWEEN %s AND %s` |\n\n`Exact` has a special `as_sql()` override: when the LHS is a boolean conditional expression and the RHS is `True`/`False`, it emits `WHERE boolean_field` or `WHERE NOT boolean_field` instead of `WHERE boolean_field = True`.\n\n`In.process_rhs()` handles the case where `rhs` is a `QuerySet` (subquery), emitting `col IN (SELECT ...)` instead of `col IN (%s, %s, ...)`.\n\n---\n\n## Stage 9: The `RegisterLookupMixin` and Lookup Resolution\n\n### `RegisterLookupMixin` \u2014 `django/db/models/query_utils.py:261`\n\nAll `Field` classes inherit from `RegisterLookupMixin`. This mixin provides the lookup registry:\n\n```python\n@functools.cache\ndef get_class_lookups(cls):\n    class_lookups = [\n        parent.__dict__.get(\"class_lookups\", {}) for parent in inspect.getmro(cls)\n    ]\n    return cls.merge_dicts(class_lookups)\n\ndef get_lookup(self, lookup_name):\n    found = self._get_lookup(lookup_name)\n    if found is None and hasattr(self, \"output_field\"):\n        return self.output_field.get_lookup(lookup_name)\n    if found is not None and not issubclass(found, Lookup):\n        return None\n    return found\n```\n\n`get_class_lookups()` is `@functools.cache`-decorated and walks the entire MRO of the field class, merging `class_lookups` dicts from each ancestor. This means lookups registered on `Field` (the base) are available on all field types, while lookups registered on `IntegerField` are only available on integer fields and their subclasses.\n\n`register_lookup()` (called via `@Field.register_lookup` decorator) stores the lookup class in `cls.class_lookups` and clears the cache for all subclasses.\n\n`get_lookup()` also delegates to `self.output_field.get_lookup()` when the lookup is not found directly \u2014 this is how `Transform` instances (which have an `output_field`) can chain into further lookups. For example, `__year__gt` resolves `year` as a `Transform` with `output_field=IntegerField`, then resolves `gt` on `IntegerField`.\n\n`Transform(RegisterLookupMixin, Func)` inherits from both `RegisterLookupMixin` and `Func`, making transforms both callable as SQL functions and capable of having their own lookup registries. The MRO ordering ensures `get_lookup()` checks the transform instance first, then falls back to `output_field`.\n\n---\n\n## Stage 10: Expression Resolution \u2014 `Col`, `F`, and `resolve_expression()`\n\n### `Col.as_sql()` \u2014 `django/db/models/expressions.py:1303`\n\n```python\ndef as_sql(self, compiler, connection):\n    alias, column = self.alias, self.target.column\n    identifiers = (alias, column) if alias else (column,)\n    sql = \".\".join(map(compiler.quote_name_unless_alias, identifiers))\n    return sql, []\n```\n\n`Col` is the leaf node for column references. It holds a table alias and a `Field` target. `as_sql()` produces `\"table_alias\".\"column_name\"` using `quote_name_unless_alias()`, which applies database quoting (e.g., double-quotes for PostgreSQL/SQLite, backticks for MySQL) but skips quoting for aliases that are already quoted or are subquery references.\n\n### `F.resolve_expression()` \u2014 `django/db/models/expressions.py:902`\n\n```python\ndef resolve_expression(self, query=None, allow_joins=True, reuse=None, summarize=False, for_save=False):\n    return query.resolve_ref(self.name, allow_joins, reuse, summarize)\n```\n\n`F` objects are not compiled directly. They are resolved during `build_filter()` or `resolve_expression()` calls into `Col` instances (or annotation references) by `query.resolve_ref()`. After resolution, the resulting `Col` is what gets compiled.\n\n### `BaseExpression.resolve_expression()` \u2014 `django/db/models/expressions.py:174`\n\nThe base `resolve_expression()` copies the expression and recursively resolves all source expressions. This is called during `Lookup.process_lhs()` and `Lookup.process_rhs()` to ensure all `F` references and nested expressions are resolved to concrete `Col` instances before SQL generation.\n\n### `Value.as_sql()` \u2014 `django/db/models/expressions.py:1125`\n\n`Value` wraps a Python literal. Its `as_sql()` calls `connection.ops.adapt_unknown_value()` and returns `(\"%s\", [prepared_value])`. The `%s` placeholder is what appears in the final parameterized SQL; the actual value goes into the params list passed to `cursor.execute()`.\n\n---\n\n## Stage 11: Execution \u2014 `execute_sql()` and `results_iter()`\n\n### `SQLCompiler.execute_sql()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/compiler.py:1592`\n\n```python\ndef execute_sql(self, result_type=MULTI, chunked_fetch=False, chunk_size=GET_ITERATOR_CHUNK_SIZE):\n    try:\n        sql, params = self.as_sql()\n        if not sql:\n            raise EmptyResultSet\n    except EmptyResultSet:\n        if result_type == MULTI:\n            return iter([])\n        return\n\n    cursor = self.connection.cursor()\n    try:\n        cursor.execute(sql, params)\n    except Exception:\n        cursor.close()\n        raise\n\n    if result_type == SINGLE:\n        return cursor.fetchone()[0:self.col_count]\n    if result_type == MULTI:\n        result = cursor_iter(cursor, ..., chunk_size)\n        return list(result)  # or chunked iterator\n```\n\n`execute_sql()` calls `as_sql()` to get the final SQL string and parameter list, then calls `cursor.execute(sql, params)`. The database driver substitutes `%s` placeholders with the actual parameter values, handling proper escaping. The `result_type` parameter controls the return format:\n- `MULTI`: returns rows in chunks via `cursor.fetchmany()`\n- `SINGLE`: returns one row via `cursor.fetchone()`\n- `CURSOR`: returns the raw cursor for caller-managed iteration\n- `ROW_COUNT`: returns `cursor.rowcount`\n\n### `SQLCompiler.results_iter()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/compiler.py:1562`\n\n```python\ndef results_iter(self, results=None, ...):\n    if results is None:\n        results = self.execute_sql(MULTI, ...)\n    fields = [s[0] for s in self.select[0:self.col_count]]\n    converters = self.get_converters(fields)\n    rows = chain.from_iterable(results)\n    if converters:\n        rows = self.apply_converters(rows, converters)\n    return rows\n```\n\n`results_iter()` applies field-level converters to each row. Converters are registered by field types via `Field.get_db_converters()` and `Field.from_db_value()`. For example, `DateTimeField` converts database timestamp strings to Python `datetime` objects, `JSONField` deserializes JSON strings, and `UUIDField` converts hex strings to `UUID` instances.\n\n---\n\n## Complete Dependency Chain\n\n```\nModel.objects                           # Each `.filter()`, `.exclude()`, `.order_by()`, `.annotate()` call mutates a cloned `Query` object's internal state (adding to `where`, `order_by`, `annotations`, etc.). SQL is only generated when the QuerySet is iterated, sliced, or explicitly evaluated. This allows arbitrary filter composition without database round-trips and enables query introspection (`.query` attribute, `.explain()`) before execution.\n\n### The `compile(node)` Dispatch as the Universal Compilation Interface\n\nEvery compilable object in the system \u2014 `WhereNode`, `Lookup`, `Col`, `Func`, `Join`, `OrderBy`, `Subquery` \u2014 implements `as_sql(compiler, connection)`. The `compile(node)` method is the single point through which all of them pass. This uniformity means:\n- Any expression can be nested inside any other expression.\n- Vendor dispatch is applied consistently at every level of the tree.\n- New expression types can be added without modifying the compiler.\n\n### WhereNode as a Recursive Boolean Tree\n\n`WhereNode` mirrors the structure of `Q` objects exactly: both are `tree.Node` subclasses with `connector` (AND/OR/XOR) and `negated` attributes. The `Q` tree is a user-facing representation; the `WhereNode` tree is the compiler-facing representation. `_add_q()` translates one into the other, with `build_filter()` converting each leaf `(lookup_string, value)` tuple into a `Lookup` instance.\n\n### `RegisterLookupMixin` as a Per-Field-Type Lookup Registry\n\nRather than a global lookup registry, each `Field` class maintains its own `class_lookups` dict. The MRO walk in `get_class_lookups()` means lookups registered on `Field` are inherited by all field types, while lookups registered on `IntegerField` are only available on integer fields. The `@functools.cache` decorator on `get_class_lookups()` makes repeated lookup resolution O(1) after the first call per field class.\n\n### Separation of Query Building from SQL Generation\n\n`Query` (in `sql/query.py`) is responsible for building the logical query tree: resolving field names, setting up joins, constructing `WhereNode` trees. `SQLCompiler` (in `sql/compiler.py`) is responsible for converting that tree into SQL strings. This separation means the same `Query` object can be compiled by different compilers (e.g., `SQLAggregateCompiler` for `.aggregate()` calls) without rebuilding the query tree.\n\n### Parameter Binding via `%s` Placeholders\n\nAll values are passed as parameters, never interpolated into the SQL string. `process_rhs()` returns `(\"%s\", [value])` for direct values. The database driver (e.g., `psycopg2`, `mysqlclient`, `sqlite3`) handles the actual substitution with proper escaping. This is the primary defense against SQL injection in Django's ORM.\n",

The clone ensures immutability of the original QuerySet.\n    81\t\n    82\t### `Q` \u2014 `django/db/models/query_utils.py:39`\n    83\t\n    84\t`Q` subclasses `django.utils.tree.Node`, which is a generic n-ary tree node. Each `Q` object has:\n    85\t- `children`: a list of `(lookup_string, value)` tuples or nested `Q` objects\n    86\t- `connector`: `AND`, `OR`, or `XOR`\n    87\t- `negated`: boolean\n    88\t\n    89\t```python\n    90\tclass Q(tree.Node):\n    91\t    AND = \"AND\"\n    92\t    OR = \"OR\"\n    93\t    XOR = \"XOR\"\n    94\t    default = AND\n    95\t\n    96\t    def __init__(self, *args, _connector=None, _negated=False, **kwargs):\n    97\t        super().__init__(\n    98\t            children=[*args, *sorted(kwargs.items())],\n    99\t            connector=_connector,\n   100\t            negated=_negated,\n   101\t        )\n   102\t```\n   103\t\n   104\t`Q` objects support `&`, `|`, `^`, and `~` operators, which produce new `Q` nodes with the appropriate connector or negation. This allows complex filter expressions to be composed before being handed to the query.\n   105\t\n   106\t### `Query.add_q()` \u2192 `_add_q()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/query.py:1625`\n   107\t\n   108\t```python\n   109\tdef add_q(self, q_object, reuse_all=False):\n   110\t    existing_inner = {a for a in self.alias_map if self.alias_map[a].join_type == INNER}\n   111\t    can_reuse = self.used_aliases\n   112\t    clause, _ = self._add_q(q_object, can_reuse)\n   113\t    if clause:\n   114\t        self.where.add(clause, AND)\n   115\t    self.demote_joins(existing_inner)\n   116\t```\n   117\t\n   118\t`add_q()` is the preprocessor. It calls `_add_q()` to recursively walk the `Q` tree and build a `WhereNode` subtree, then ANDs that subtree into `self.where` (the root `WhereNode` of the query). After adding, it calls `demote_joins()` to convert any OUTER JOINs that are now constrained back to INNER JOINs.\n   119\t\n   120\t```python\n   121\tdef _add_q(self, q_object, used_aliases, branch_negated=False,\n   122\t           current_negated=False, allow_joins=True, ...):\n   123\t    connector = q_object.connector\n   124\t    current_negated ^= q_object.negated\n   125\t    target_clause = WhereNode(connector=connector, negated=q_object.negated)\n   126\t    for child in q_object.children:\n   127\t        child_clause, needed_inner = self.build_filter(child, ...)\n   128\t        if child_clause:\n   129\t            target_clause.add(child_clause, connector)\n   130\t    return target_clause, needed_inner\n   131\t```\n   132\t\n   133\t`_add_q()` creates a new `WhereNode` mirroring the `Q` node's connector and negation, then calls `build_filter()` for each child, adding the resulting `WhereNode` clauses as children.\n   134\t\n   135\t### `Query.build_filter()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/query.py:1460`\n   136\t\n   137\tThis is the core of filter-to-SQL-tree translation. For a `(lookup_string, value)` tuple like `('author__name__icontains', 'Django')`:\n   138\t\n   139\t1. **`solve_lookup_type(arg)`** \u2014 splits `'author__name__icontains'` into field parts `['author', 'name']` and lookup parts `['icontains']` by walking `names_to_path()` against the model's `_meta`.\n   140\t\n   141\t2. **`setup_joins(parts, opts, alias)`** \u2014 traverses the field path, creating JOIN entries in `query.alias_map` for each relation traversed. Returns `join_info` containing the final field, alias, and join list.\n   142\t\n   143\t3. **`trim_joins()`** \u2014 removes unnecessary joins (e.g., when joining to a primary key that can be resolved on the parent table).\n   144\t\n   145\t4. **`_get_col(target, field, alias)`** \u2014 creates a `Col(alias, target)` expression representing the left-hand side of the lookup.\n   146\t\n   147\t5. **`build_lookup(lookups, col, value)`** \u2014 resolves the lookup class and instantiates it:\n   148\t\n   149\t```python\n   150\tdef build_lookup(self, lookups, lhs, rhs):\n   151\t    *transforms, lookup_name = lookups or [\"exact\"]\n   152\t    for name in transforms:\n   153\t        lhs = self.try_transform(lhs, name, lookups)\n   154\t    lookup_class = lhs.get_lookup(lookup_name)\n   155\t    lookup = lookup_class(lhs, rhs)\n   156\t    return lookup\n   157\t```\n   158\t\n   159\tTransforms (e.g., `__year`, `__month`) are applied first, each wrapping the previous `lhs` in a `Transform` instance. Then the final lookup class is retrieved via `RegisterLookupMixin.get_lookup()` and instantiated with `(lhs, rhs)`.\n   160\t\n   161\t6. It:\n   197\t1. Calls `query.get_compiler()` to obtain the appropriate `SQLCompiler` instance.\n   198\t2. Calls `compiler.execute_sql()` to run the query and get raw rows.\n   199\t3. This call resolves the compiler class by name from the backend's compiler module and instantiates it with the query, connection, database alias, and `elide_empty` flag.\n   216\t\n   217\t### `BaseDatabaseOperations.compiler()` \u2014 `django/db/backends/base/operations.py:385`\n   218\t\n   219\t```python\n   220\tcompiler_module = \"django.db.models.sql.compiler\"\n   221\t\n   222\tdef compiler(self, compiler_name):\n   223\t    if self._cache is None:\n   224\t        self._cache = import_module(self.compiler_module)\n   225\t    return getattr(self._cache, compiler_name)\n   226\t```\n   227\t\n   228\tThe `compiler_module` attribute is the extension point for backend-specific compilers:\n   229\t\n   230\t| Backend | `vendor` | `compiler_module` |\n   231\t|---|---|---|\n   232\t| SQLite | `\"sqlite\"` | `\"django.db.models.sql.compiler\"` (default) |\n   233\t| PostgreSQL | `\"postgresql\"` | `\"django.db.backends.postgresql.compiler\"` |\n   234\t| MySQL | `\"mysql\"` | `\"django.db.backends.mysql.compiler\"` |\n   235\t| Oracle | `\"oracle\"` | `\"django.db.models.sql.compiler\"` (default) |\n   236\t\n   237\tPostgreSQL overrides `SQLInsertCompiler` to use `UNNEST` for bulk inserts. MySQL overrides `SQLDeleteCompiler` and `SQLUpdateCompiler` to handle multi-table delete/update syntax. SQLite and Oracle use the base compiler for all operations, relying instead on vendor dispatch within expressions.\n   238\t\n   239\t---\n   240\t\n   241\t## Stage 5: SQL Assembly \u2014 `SQLCompiler.as_sql()`\n   242\t\n   243\t### `SQLCompiler.__init__()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/compiler.py`\n   244\t\n   245\t```python\n   246\tdef __init__(self, query, connection, using, elide_empty=True):\n   247\t    self.query = query\n   248\t    self.connection = connection\n   249\t    self.using = using\n   250\t    self.elide_empty = elide_empty\n   251\t    self.quote_cache = {\"*\": \"*\"}\n   252\t    self.select = None\n   253\t    self.annotation_col_map = None\n   254\t    self.klass_info = None\n   255\t    self.where = None\n   256\t    self.having = None\n   257\t    self.qualify = None\n   258\t```\n   259\t\n   260\t### `SQLCompiler.as_sql()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/compiler.py:754`\n   261\t\n   262\t`as_sql()` orchestrates the full SQL string assembly:\n   263\t\n   264\t```python\n   265\tdef as_sql(self, with_limits=True, with_col_aliases=False):\n   266\t    extra_select, order_by, group_by = self.pre_sql_setup(...)\n   267\t    ...\n   268\t    distinct_fields, distinct_params = self.get_distinct()\n   269\t    from_, f_params = self.get_from_clause()\n   270\t    where, w_params = self.compile(self.where) if self.where else (\"\", [])\n   271\t    having, h_params = self.compile(self.having) if self.having else (\"\", [])\n   272\t\n   273\t    result = [\"SELECT\"]\n   274\t    result += [\", \".join(out_cols)]   # ON ...` and bare table name strings respectively.\n   291\t\n   292\t**`get_select()`** compiles each column expression via `compile(col)`, where `col` is typically a `Col` instance. The result is a list of `(expression, (sql, params), alias)` triples stored in `self.select`.\n   293\t\n   294\t---\n   295\t\n   296\t## Stage 6: The `compile(node)` Vendor Dispatch Mechanism\n   297\t\n   298\t### `SQLCompiler.compile()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/compiler.py:571`\n   299\t\n   300\t```python\n   301\tdef compile(self, node):\n   302\t    vendor_impl = getattr(node, \"as_\" + self.connection.vendor, None)\n   303\t    if vendor_impl:\n   304\t        sql, params = vendor_impl(self, self.connection)\n   305\t    else:\n   306\t        sql, params = node.as_sql(self, self.connection)\n   307\t    return sql, params\n   308\t```\n   309\t\n   310\tThis is the central dispatch point of the entire compilation pipeline. Every node in the expression tree \u2014 `WhereNode`, `Lookup`, `Col`, `Func`, `Join`, `OrderBy`, etc. \u2014 is compiled through this single method.\n   311\t\n   312\t**Dispatch logic:**\n   313\t1. Check if the node has a method named `as_{vendor}` (e.g., `as_sqlite`, `as_postgresql`, `as_mysql`, `as_oracle`).\n   314\t2. If found, call it with `(compiler, connection)` \u2014 the vendor-specific implementation takes precedence.\n   315\t3. If not found, fall back to the generic `node.as_sql(compiler, connection)`.\n   316\t\n   317\tThis design allows any expression class to provide backend-specific SQL generation by simply defining an `as_{vendor}` method, without requiring subclassing of the compiler or any central dispatch table. The compiler itself remains backend-agnostic.\n   318\t\n   319\t**Examples of vendor-specific implementations:**\n   320\t\n   321\t- `SQLiteNumericMixin.as_sqlite()` (`expressions.py:22`): Wraps `DecimalField` expressions in `CAST(... AS NUMERIC)` because SQLite stores decimals as text and needs explicit casting for arithmetic.\n   322\t- `Lookup.as_oracle()` (`lookups.py:152`): Wraps boolean expressions in `CASE WHEN ... THEN True ELSE False END` because Oracle does not support boolean expressions directly in WHERE clauses.\n   323\t- `Func` subclasses in `django/db/models/functions/`: Many database functions have vendor-specific SQL syntax (e.g., `NOW()` vs `GETDATE()`, `SUBSTR` vs `SUBSTRING`).\n   324\t- JSON field lookups in `django/db/models/fields/json.py`: All four vendor methods are defined because JSON path syntax differs significantly across databases.\n   325\t\n   326\t---\n   327\t\n   328\t## Stage 7: WhereNode and Expression Tree Compilation\n   329\t\n   330\t### `WhereNode.as_sql()` \u2014 `django/db/models/sql/where.py:116`\n   331\t\n   332\t```python\n   333\tdef as_sql(self, compiler, connection):\n   334\t    result = []\n   335\t    result_params = []\n   336\t    if self.connector == AND:\n   337\t        full_needed, empty_needed = len(self.children), 1\n   338\t    else:\n   339\t        full_needed, empty_needed = 1, len(self.children)\n   340\t\n   341\t    for child in self.children:\n   342\t        try:<response clipped><NOTE>Due to the max output limit, only part of this file has been shown to you. "SQLCompiler.results_iter()            # compiler.py:1562\n  \u2514\u2500\u2500 apply_converters(rows, converters)  # Field.from_db_value() per field\n        \u2514\u2500\u2500 yields converted row tuples\n\nModelIterable.__iter__() (continued)\n  \u2514\u2500\u2500 model_cls.from_db(db, init_list, row)  # constructs model instances\n        \u2514\u2500\u2500 yields model objects \u2192 QuerySet._result_cache\n```\n\n---\n\n## Architectural Patterns and Design Decisions\n\n### Lazy Evaluation via Deferred SQL Generation\n\nThe QuerySet never generates SQL during filter chaining. Each `.filter()`, `.exclude()`, `.order_by()`, `.annotate()` call mutates a cloned `Query` object's internal state (adding to `where`, `order_by`, `annotations`, etc.). SQL is only generated when the QuerySet is iterated, sliced, or explicitly evaluated. This allows arbitrary filter composition without database round-trips and enables query introspection (`.query` attribute, `.explain()`) before execution.\n\n### The `compile(node)` Dispatch as the Universal Compilation Interface\n\nEvery compilable object in the system \u2014 `WhereNode`, `Lookup`, `Col`, `Func`, `Join`, `OrderBy`, `Subquery` \u2014 implements `as_sql(compiler, connection)`. The `compile(node)` method is the single point through which all of them pass. This uniformity means:\n- Any expression can be nested inside any other expression.\n- Vendor dispatch is applied consistently at every level of the tree.\n- New expression types can be added without modifying the compiler.\n\n### WhereNode as a Recursive Boolean Tree\n\n`WhereNode` mirrors the structure of `Q` objects exactly: both are `tree.Node` subclasses with `connector` (AND/OR/XOR) and `negated` attributes. The `Q` tree is a user-facing representation; the `WhereNode` tree is the compiler-facing representation. `_add_q()` translates one into the other, with `build_filter()` converting each leaf `(lookup_string, value)` tuple into a `Lookup` instance.\n\n### `RegisterLookupMixin` as a Per-Field-Type Lookup Registry\n\nRather than a global lookup registry, each `Field` class maintains its own `class_lookups` dict. The MRO walk in `get_class_lookups()` means lookups registered on `Field` are inherited by all field types, while lookups registered on `IntegerField` are only available on integer fields. The `@functools.cache` decorator on `get_class_lookups()` makes repeated lookup resolution O(1) after the first call per field class.\n\n### Separation of Query Building from SQL Generation\n\n`Query` (in `sql/query.py`) is responsible for building the logical query tree: resolving field names, setting up joins, constructing `WhereNode` trees. `SQLCompiler` (in `sql/compiler.py`) is responsible for converting that tree into SQL strings. This separation means the same `Query` object can be compiled by different compilers (e.g., `SQLAggregateCompiler` for `.aggregate()` calls) without rebuilding the query tree.\n\n### Parameter Binding via `%s` Placeholders\n\nAll values are passed as parameters, never interpolated into the SQL string. `process_rhs()` returns `(\"%s\", [value])` for direct values. The database driver (e.g., `psycopg2`, `mysqlclient`, `sqlite3`) handles the actual substitution with proper escaping. This is the primary defense against SQL injection in Django's ORM."
